<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="2014f8ea580245e48704ebba0ca334c3"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="2014f8ea580245e48704ebba0ca334c3">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '66ef49cd-5a47-4f85-8c6c-3c77ec9e9e1d'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <h1 class="citation__title" lang="en">Bi@C nanosphere anode with Na<sup>+</sup>-ether-solvent cointercalation behavior to achieve fast sodium storage under extreme low temperatures</h1>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="publicaitonContent-versions"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   <div class="loa comma visible-xs mobile-authors loa-authors-trunc">
      <div class="comma__list"><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Liu/Lingli" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="am1" data-db-target-for="am1" aria-controls="am1" aria-haspopup="true" id="am1_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Lingli Liu</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="am1" aria-labelledby="am1_Ctrl" role="region" id="am1">

                     <p>Institute for Carbon Neutralization, College of Chemistry and Materials Engineering, Wenzhou University, Wenzhou, China</p>
                     <p><b>Correspondence</b> Xin Liang, School of Energy, Materials and Chemical Engineering, Hefei University, 230601 Hefei, China.</p>
                     <p>Email: <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:x.liang@hfuu.edu.cn"><span>x.liang@hfuu.edu.cn</span></a></p>
                     <p>Yongsheng Han, State Key Laboratory of Multiphase Complex Systems, Institute of Process Engineering, Chinese Academy of Sciences, 100190 Beijing, China.</p>
                     <p>Email: <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:yshan@ipe.ac.cn"><span>yshan@ipe.ac.cn</span></a></p>
                     <p>Shulei Chou, Institute for Carbon Neutralization, College of Chemistry and Materials Engineering, Wenzhou University, 325035 Wenzhou, China.</p>
                     <p>Email: <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:chou@wzh.edu.cn"><span>chou@wzh.edu.cn</span></a></p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Chou/Shulei">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span></div>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d66667790" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>The low ion transport is a major obstacle for low-temperature (LT) sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). Herein, a core-shell structure of bismuth (Bi) nanospheres coated with carbon (Bi@C) is constructed by utilizing a novel Bi-based complex (1,4,5,8-naphthalenetetracarboxylic dianhydride as the ligand) as the precursor, which provides an effective template to fabricate Bi-based anodes. At −40°C, the Bi@C anode achieves a high capacity, which is equivalent to 96% of that at 25°C, benefitting from the core-shell nanostructured engineering and Na<sup>+</sup>-ether-solvent cointercalation process. The special Na<sup>+</sup>-diglyme cointercalation behavior may effectively reduce the activation energy and accelerate the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics, enabling the excellent low-temperature performance of the Bi@C electrode. As expected, the fabricated Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>//Bi@C full-cell delivers impressive rechargeability in the ether-based electrolyte at −40°C. Density functional theory calculations and electrochemical tests also reveal the fast reaction kinetic mechanism at LT, thanks to a much lower diffusion energy barrier (167 meV) and a lower reaction activation energy (32.2 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>) of Bi@C anode in comparison with that of bulk Bi. This work provides a rational design of Bi-based electrodes for rechargeable SIBs under extreme conditions.</p></div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cey2531-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cey2531-sec-0010-title">1 INTRODUCTION</h2>
         
         <p>Enhancing the low-temperature (LT) tolerance of rechargeable batteries in a cold climate is necessary for electric vehicles, grid storage, national defense security, space exploration, and deep-sea operations.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0001_R_d66667777e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv Energy Mater">1</a>-<a href="#cey2531-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0003_R_d66667777e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Energy Environ Sci">3</a></sup></span> The capacity and power fading of lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) at ultra-LTs still limits their application despite that the LIBs have been universally adopted for all kinds of electronics.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0001_R_d66667777e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv Energy Mater">1</a>, <a href="#cey2531-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0004_R_d66667777e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Energy Environ Sci">4</a></sup></span> A good performance of commercial LIBs based on ethylene carbonate electrolytes and graphite anodes can only be achieved at above −20°C, which restricts their applications in harsh environments.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0004_R_d66667777e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Energy Environ Sci">4</a>, <a href="#cey2531-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0005_R_d66667777e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - J Power Sources">5</a></sup></span> Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) represent a promising alternative due to their low cost, abundance of sodium resources, and safety.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0006_R_d66667777e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv Mater">6</a>-<a href="#cey2531-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0010_R_d66667777e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv Funct Mater">10</a></sup></span> More significantly, Na<sup>+</sup> exhibits a desolvation energy between Na<sup>+</sup> and various organic solvents that is ∼25% lower than that of Li<sup>+</sup> in a range of organic solvents. Besides, the Stokes radius of Na<sup>+</sup> (4.6 Å) is smaller than that of Li<sup>+</sup> (4.8 Å), indicating that the electrolyte with the same concentration has a higher ionic conductivity than the lithium salt electrolyte or the electrolyte with a lower concentration can achieve the same ionic conductivity.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0011_R_d66667777e519" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J Electrochem Soc">11</a>, <a href="#cey2531-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0012_R_d66667777e519" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - ACS Omega">12</a></sup></span> As a result, these above factors endow SIBs with relatively superior LT performances compared to LIBs.</p>
         
         <p>At present, the main issues hindering the performance of SIBs at LT need to be addressed as follows: (i) The electrolyte tends to solidify when the temperature drops below 0°C. The situation results in a lower wettability and ionic conductivity of the electrolyte, which affects the ion transport rate in the electrolyte.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0013_R_d66667777e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - ACS Energy Lett">13</a>, <a href="#cey2531-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0014_R_d66667777e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - ACS Appl Mater Interfaces">14</a></sup></span> (ii) Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation reaction becomes difficult at LT, which leads to a high charge transfer resistance.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0015_R_d66667777e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Angew Chem Int Ed">15</a></sup></span> The impeded kinetics, including ion diffusion within the electrolyte and electrode materials and charge transfer across the electrode–electrolyte interfaces, all results in poor LT performance; the exact reasons of impeded kinetics at LT were not actually clear. (iii) Na<sup>+</sup> may drag through the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) at LT, which results in the reduced migration rates of Na<sup>+</sup> in the electrode material and resultant sluggish kinetics.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0016_R_d66667777e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Angew Chem Int Ed">16</a></sup></span> Therefore, future missions with developing advanced materials, adaptable electrolyte systems, and processes suitable for LT SIBs are major challenges to the development of SIBs, which have been a cornerstone of the state of the art.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0017_R_d66667777e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv Mater">17</a>-<a href="#cey2531-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0019_R_d66667777e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ACS Sustainable Chem Eng">19</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>The alloy-based anodes for SIBs have attracted much attention on account of their higher capacity, suitable working potential, and earth abundance.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0020_R_d66667777e546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv Mater">20</a>-<a href="#cey2531-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0023_R_d66667777e546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Energy Storage Mater">23</a></sup></span> As illustrated in the previous research, the alloy-based anodes showed safe sodiation potentials in an LT range and large theoretical capacities of larger than 300 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> based on an alloying/dealloying mechanism.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0017_R_d66667777e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv Mater">17</a>, <a href="#cey2531-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0020_R_d66667777e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv Mater">20</a>, <a href="#cey2531-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0021_R_d66667777e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Sustainable Chem Eng">21</a>, <a href="#cey2531-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0024_R_d66667777e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv Energy Mater">24</a>-<a href="#cey2531-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0027_R_d66667777e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Small">27</a></sup></span> Bismuth (Bi) has been demonstrated as a promising anode for SIBs because of its high theoretical gravimetry (386 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and volumetric capacity (3765 mAh cm<sup>−3</sup>) and acceptable sodium alloy reaction potential at a voltage of 0.5 V. For example, bulk Bi//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP)-carbon nanotube (CNTs) presented outstanding rate performance for Na storage from −15°C to 45°C. The full battery delivered an impressive rate capacity with 255 at 4000 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0024_R_d66667777e569" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv Energy Mater">24</a></sup></span> The excellent performance is contributed to the gradually formed three-dimensional porous networks of Bi anode, and the facile redox reaction kinetics and fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in both the Bi anode and NVP-CNTs cathode, as well as the stable SEI film and cross-linked NVP with CNTs. Despite bulk Bi anode exhibiting significant function in the Bi//NVP-CNTs full-cell system, the inevitable pulverization of Bi anodes results from large volume expansion (≈244%) during sodiation, which affects the long-cycle performances and rate capabilities.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0026_R_d66667777e575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Carbon">26</a></sup></span> Structural design for the LT tolerance of rechargeable batteries is necessary to realize unique physicochemical properties and achieve fast ion transport or shortened ion diffusion distances. Besides, the sodium-storage mechanisms of impeded kinetics and the structure–activity relationship of Bi-based anodes at LT need to be further explored. These motivate us to construct a delicate structure for a Bi-based anode along with an applied electrolyte and investigate the sodium-storage mechanisms at LT to realize outstanding LT SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>The density functional theory (DFT) theoretical simulations have demonstrated the higher adsorption energy and the lower diffusion energy barrier of Bi/C materials in comparison to bulk Bi, which presents an absolute superiority to bulk Bi. Herein, an appropriate precursor of the Bi-NTCDA complex was eventually discovered to fabricate carbon-coated nanosphere Bi (Bi@C) anode in this work to improve its physicochemical properties and suppress volume changes of Bi anodes during sodiation–desodiation. The delicate core-shell nanostructure and high-dispersed nanospheres of Bi@C (∼50 nm) demonstrate notable stability, high-rate capability, and outstanding LT performances in the NaPF<sub>6</sub>/diglyme electrolyte in comparison to bulk Bi. More significantly, the rational electrolyte ensures Na<sup>+</sup>-diglyme cointercalation into the Bi@C electrode to overcome the kinetic barriers of rechargeable SIBs at extremely LTs. As expected, both the experiment and calculations have confirmed the superiority of Bi@C anode, which overcomes the impeded kinetics of SIBs at LT. More significantly, the proposed Bi@C//NVP full battery also shows notable stability and operates well even at extreme LT.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cey2531-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cey2531-sec-0020-title">2 EXPERIMENTAL SECTION</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cey2531-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cey2531-sec-0030-title">2.1 Materials preparation</h3>
            
            <p>Bi nitrate pentahydrate (Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O, AR, 99.0%) and 1,4,5,8-naphthalenetetracarboxylic dianhydride (NTCDA, 96%) were purchased from Shanghai Macklin Co., Ltd. Sodium hydroxide (NaOH, 97%) and Bi powder (Bi, 99.9%) were purchased from Shanghai Aladdin Biochemical Technology Co., Ltd. NVP is commercial powder. NaPF<sub>6</sub> dissolved in diglyme formed a 1 M electrolyte solution and was purchased from Suzhou Dodo Chemical Technology Co., Ltd. The Bi@C was firstly synthesized from Bi nitrate pentahydrate and NTCDA via the chemical precipitation method as follows. For the synthesis of Bi@C nanospheres, NaOH (36 mg) and 1,4,5,8-NTCDA (120 mg) were first dissolved in 60 mL of deionized water in a 100 mL beaker, and then the mixture solution was stirred at 60°C for 0.5 h. After the addition of Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O (485 mg), the resulting mixture was stirred at 60°C for 24 h.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0028_R_d66667777e614" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Appl Mater Interfaces">28</a></sup></span> Then, the obtained product was centrifuged, washed several times with deionized water and ethanol, and then dried in a desiccator at 80°C for 12 h. Finally, the dried product was annealed at 600°C for 2 h in a 5% Ar/H<sub>2</sub> atmosphere with a heating rate of 2°C min<sup>−1</sup>. After being naturally cooled to room temperature, Bi@C nanospheres were collected. For comparison, bulk Bi is commercial Bi powder.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cey2531-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cey2531-sec-0040-title">2.2 Materials characterization</h3>
            
            <p>The crystal structure of the products was analyzed by powder X-ray diffraction (XRD; Rigaku Smartlab SE) with Cu Kα radiation (<i>λ</i> = 0.15406 nm). The dimensions, morphologies, and structures of the products were observed through a field emission scanning electron microscope (SEM; SU8010 Hitachi) and transmission electron microscope (TEM, JEOL, JEM-2100 with energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy). X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) data were collected on an&nbsp;XPS (ESCA, 2000; VG, Microtech) using an Al X-ray source (2 kV, 1486.6 eV). Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) was acquired using a thermogravimetric analyzer (STA 449 F5; Netzsch) with a heating rate of 10°C min<sup>−1</sup> from room temperature to 800°C in the air atmosphere. The specific surface area and pore size were identified based on the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller method and BJH analysis of the desorption branches of the isotherm, respectively (Micromeritics ASAP 2020 Plus HD88). In situ, XRD measurement was conducted in a Swagelok cell equipped with aluminum as an X-ray window on the anode side with a current density of 38 mA g<sup>−1</sup> from 0.01 to 1.5 V used to determine an alloying/dealloying reaction mechanism. The contact angle was measured using a&nbsp;contact angle meter (JC2000DM; Shanghai Zhongchen Digital Technology Apparatus Co., Ltd.).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cey2531-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cey2531-sec-0050-title">2.3 Electrochemical measurements</h3>
            
            <p>The sodium storage performance was tested in CR2032 coin cells assembled in a glove box filled with Ar atmosphere (the content of H<sub>2</sub>O and O<sub>2</sub> less than 1 ppm). Bi@C electrodes were prepared by mixing the active materials (Bi@C or bulk Bi), Super P, and carboxymethyl cellulose in a solvent of deionized water at a mass ratio of 8:1:1. The mixed slurry was uniformly pasted on the Cu foil and then dried at 100°C for 12 h in a vacuum oven. The mass loading of the active material on the electrode was about 1.0 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. Specifically, the precycle treatment of the Bi@C anode was cycled at a current density of 200 mA g<sup>−1</sup> for five cycles before a long-cycle test. NVP cathode was made by mixing the NVP powder, Super P, and polyvinylidene fluoride in a weight ratio of 7:2:1 and dispersing the mixture in <i>N</i>-methyl pyrrolidone. The slurry was cast onto Al foil and dried at 100°C for 12 h in a vacuum oven. The areal loading of NVP material was about 5 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. For the half-cell measurement, the glass fiber (GF/D; Whatman) was used as the separator, Na metal served as counter and reference electrodes, and the electrolyte was 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in diglyme. Galvanostatic charge/discharge tests were performed in a voltage range of 0.01–1.5 V for Bi@C//Na and 2.0–3.8 V for NVP//Na at different current densities on the Land CT2001A battery system. To test the Bi@C//NVP full-cell performance, the Bi@C anode and NVP cathode were paired up in a weight ratio of 1:4 via CR2032-type coin cells. The electrolyte and separator were the same as that used in the half battery. The Bi@C//NVP full cell was tested between 1.6 and 3.2 V. Specifically, before LT tests, the Bi@C cell was first cycled at 25°C for 10 cycles. Then, the Bi@C cell was further tested at different LTs. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) was tested on a CHI1760E electrochemical workstation between 0.01 and 1.5 V at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. Electrochemical impedance spectrum measurements were performed on the CHI1760E electrochemical workstation in the frequency range from 100 kHz to 0.01 Hz. For testing at LT, it is also necessary to use the humidity chamber (MSK-TH150-60CP; Shenzhen Kejing Start Technology Co., Ltd.) to set various temperatures.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cey2531-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cey2531-sec-0060-title">2.4 Theoretical simulations</h3>
            
            <p>The first-principle calculation was performed in the framework of DFT as implemented in the VASP program.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0029_R_d66667777e671" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Phys Rev B">29</a></sup></span> The generalized gradient approximation proposed by Perdew, Burke, and Ernzerhof is selected for the exchange-correlation potential.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0030_R_d66667777e674" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J Chem Phys">30</a></sup></span> The cut-off energy for the plane wave is set to 450 eV. The energy criterion is set to 10−5 eV in the iterative solution of the Kohn–Sham equation. For all models, the vacuum space along the <i>z</i>-direction was set to be 15 Å, which was enough to avoid interaction between the two neighboring images. The Brillouin zone integration is performed using a 3 × 3 × 1 k-mesh. All the structures are relaxed until the residual forces on the atoms have declined to less than 0.02 eV/Å. The van der Waals dispersion-corrected DFT was also carried out, as proposed by Grimme et al.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0030_R_d66667777e679" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J Chem Phys">30</a></sup></span> The barriers for Na diffusion are calculated with the climbing-image nudged elastic band method.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0031_R_d66667777e682" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J Chem Phys">31</a></sup></span></p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cey2531-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cey2531-sec-0070-title">3 RESULTS AND DISCUSSIONS</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cey2531-sec-0080">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cey2531-sec-0080-title">3.1 Structural regulation</h3>
            
            <p>In terms of the superiority of Bi anode along with the large volume changes in electrode materials, a specific nanostructure with Bi as the “core” and carbon as the “shell” was rationally designed in this work. Bi@C nanospheres featuring a core-shell structure were synthesized from Bi nitrate and NTCDA via facile chemical deposition process and carbonization, as schematically shown in Figure <a href="#cey2531-fig-0001">1A</a>. The morphology and structure of the Bi@C core-shell nanospheres were investigated by using SEM and TEM (Figure <a href="#cey2531-fig-0001">1B–D</a>). As anticipated, the spherical morphology of the precursor was successfully preserved after high-temperature carbonization, as evidenced by Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1–S3</a>. The TEM images depicted in Figure <a href="#cey2531-fig-0001">1B,C</a> confirmed that the Bi@C core-shell nanospheres (∼50 nm) were encapsulated by a carbon layer with a thickness of approximately 10 nm. Evidently, the TEM images showed that a carbon layer was formed in the outer layer and wrapped around the Bi crystals, but bulk Bi shows a large block morphology (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>). Additionally, a distinct lattice fringe of 0.225 nm was detected via fast Fourier transformation simulation of Digital Micrograph software (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>), which corresponds to the interplane spacing of the (110) crystal lattice for metal Bi (Figure <a href="#cey2531-fig-0001">1D</a>). The elemental mappings (Figure <a href="#cey2531-fig-0001">1E–G</a>) revealed uniform distributions of Bi and C elements in the Bi@C core-shell nanospheres, suggesting that Bi crystals were homogenously coated within the carbon layer.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cey2531-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f55347ad-a5a3-4418-803e-eed820df02b5/cey2531-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/f55347ad-a5a3-4418-803e-eed820df02b5/cey2531-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/007a2233-141a-4f7f-9295-a4b62e0a1d2c/cey2531-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f55347ad-a5a3-4418-803e-eed820df02b5/cey2531-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cey2531-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcey2.531" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">(A) Schematic illustration of the synthesis of Bi@C core-shell nanospheres. (B–D) TEM images of Bi@C nanospheres. (E–G) Elemental mappings of Bi@C core-shell nanospheres.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The crystal structure of the Bi@C nanospheres was further verified via XRD patterns, revealing a high-crystallinity rhombohedral Bi phase (JCPDS:01-085-1329) after annealing, as shown in Figure <a href="#cey2531-fig-0002">2A</a>. The content of Bi in Bi@C was estimated at 87.6% as calculated from TGA plots (Figure <a href="#cey2531-fig-0002">2B</a> and Equation <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>), taking into account the oxidation of Bi to Bi<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> and the combustion of carbon between 150°C and 500°C.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0032_R_d66667777e761" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J Mater Chem A">32</a>, <a href="#cey2531-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0033_R_d66667777e761" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Angew Chem Int Ed">33</a></sup></span> To accurately evaluate the pore structure of Bi@C, the N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption method was carried out, revealing a specific surface area and pore volume of 17 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> and 0.11 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>). To further identify the elemental composition and chemical states of carbon (C), oxygen (O), and Bi in as-prepared Bi@C, the XPS analysis was implemented. The full spectrum analysis revealed the presence of Bi, C, and O elements (Figure <a href="#cey2531-fig-0002">2C</a>).<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0034_R_d66667777e781" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv Funct Mater">34</a></sup></span> The high-resolution spectrum of Bi 4f (Figure <a href="#cey2531-fig-0002">2D</a>) displayed two peaks at 158.2 and 163.4 eV, corresponding to Bi 4f<sub>7/2</sub> and Bi 4f<sub>5/2</sub>, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0035_R_d66667777e792" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Small">35</a>, <a href="#cey2531-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0036_R_d66667777e792" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - App Mater Today">36</a></sup></span> In the high-resolution C 1s spectrum (Figure <a href="#cey2531-fig-0002">2E</a>), three peaks were observed at 284.8, 286.3, and 289.1 eV, which were attributed to C–C, C–O, and C═O bond, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0037_R_d66667777e798" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Energy Storage Mater">37</a>, <a href="#cey2531-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0038_R_d66667777e798" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv Mater">38</a></sup></span> The spectrum of O 1s (Figure <a href="#cey2531-fig-0002">2F</a>) exhibited three fitting peaks at 529.9, 530.9, and 533.6 eV, corresponding to the Bi–O, C–O–Bi, and C–O bond, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0039_R_d66667777e804" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J Energy Chem">39</a>, <a href="#cey2531-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0040_R_d66667777e804" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Small">40</a></sup></span> The presence of the Bi–O bond may be attributed to the unavoidable oxidation of highly active Bi in the nanoscale during the sample preparation and storage.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0041_R_d66667777e807" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv Mater">41</a>, <a href="#cey2531-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0042_R_d66667777e807" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Nanoscale">42</a></sup></span> Furthermore, the interaction between Bi, O, and C may contribute to stabilizing the Bi nanospheres and ensuring the structural stability of the Bi@C nanospheres.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cey2531-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9ec4d9b0-0898-46cf-a324-6c3b1b34674c/cey2531-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/9ec4d9b0-0898-46cf-a324-6c3b1b34674c/cey2531-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8e76932f-d3d4-4b0d-8114-d38d4bc67515/cey2531-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9ec4d9b0-0898-46cf-a324-6c3b1b34674c/cey2531-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cey2531-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcey2.531" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">(A) XRD pattern, (B) TGA, and (C–F) XPS spectra of the Bi@C core-shell nanospheres.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cey2531-sec-0090">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cey2531-sec-0090-title">3.2 Electrochemical performance</h3>
            
            <p>The mechanism of Na storage in the Bi@C electrode was investigated by analyzing the CV response (Figure <a href="#cey2531-fig-0003">3A</a>). The first three cycles of CV tests were performed on the Bi@C electrode with a scanning speed of 0.1 mV<sup>−1</sup> in the voltage range of 0.01–1.5 V. During the initial cycle, a cathode peak was observed at 1.03 V, which disappeared completely during the following cycles, indicating the irreversible decomposition of the electrolyte at the electrode–electrolyte interface, indicating the SEI formation.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0038_R_d66667777e844" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv Mater">38</a></sup></span> Two cathodic peaks at 0.44 and 0.64 V are assigned to the two-step alloy reactions. Accordingly, the anodic peaks at 0.63 and 0.77 V are related to the stepwise dealloying reactions.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0041_R_d66667777e847" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv Mater">41</a>, <a href="#cey2531-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0043_R_d66667777e847" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Adv Mater">43</a></sup></span> Furthermore, the two pairs of cathodic/anodic peaks showed a high coincidence in the subsequent cycle, exhibiting excellent electrochemical reversibility and cycle stability. The charge/discharge curves of Bi@C at a current density of 200 mA g<sup>−1</sup> are presented in Figure <a href="#cey2531-fig-0003">3B</a>. In the first cycle, voltage plateau of 1.03 V in the discharge curve appeared again and disappeared in the following cycles. The distinct voltage plateaus in the charge/discharge curves are totally consistent with the results of the CV curves.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cey2531-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2ef0b7c7-28d1-4807-bac8-392fc3039b0a/cey2531-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/2ef0b7c7-28d1-4807-bac8-392fc3039b0a/cey2531-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/949f632c-519b-4511-8422-946e505e0c24/cey2531-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2ef0b7c7-28d1-4807-bac8-392fc3039b0a/cey2531-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cey2531-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcey2.531" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of the Bi@C electrode. (A) CV curves at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> between 0.01 and 1.5 V at 25°C. (B) Charge/discharge profiles at a current density of 500 mA g<sup>−1</sup> at 25°C. (C) Cycling performances comparison between the bulk Bi and Bi@C anodes at 500 mA g<sup>−1</sup> at 25°C. (D) Rate performances of Bi@C anode compared with bulk Bi for SIBs at 25°C. (E) Cycling performance at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> at 25°C. (F) Charge/discharge profiles of Bi@C anodes at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> at −40°C. (G) Charge/discharge profiles of Bi@C anodes at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> at different temperatures. (H) Rate performance of Bi@C anodes under different temperatures at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>To investigate the sodium storage behavior on Bi@C and bulk Bi, galvanostatic measurements of sodium half-cells were conducted. The first five cycles of each cell were activated at a low current density of 200 mA g<sup>−1</sup>; the measurements of cycle life were then performed, as shown in Figure <a href="#cey2531-fig-0003">3C</a>. Bi@C electrodes have a capacity of 339 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles at the same current density with bulk Bi, with a capacity retention rate of 96.7%. Moreover, as displayed in Figure <a href="#cey2531-fig-0003">3D</a>, the Bi@C electrode delivers much superior rate capabilities than the bulk Bi electrode. The Bi@C anode material demonstrated reversible capacity of 331, 343, 343, 341, 338, and 327 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the current densities of 0.1, 0.3, 0.5, 1, 2, and 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Only a 1.2%&nbsp; of capacity loss was achieved when the current densities increased from 0.1 to 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, while the bulk Bi electrode presented a 24% capacity loss. As demonstrated in Figures <a href="#cey2531-fig-0003">3E</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, the Bi@C anode also exhibited remarkable stability and long-cycle behavior as well as a capacity of 301 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even over 2000 cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> achieved, while bulk Bi showed only 203 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> over 303 cycles. The cells assembled with bulk Bi electrodes showed the overcharging phenomenon, which may be due to the volume expansion caused by the alloying reaction and the structural collapse of the electrodes. Accordingly, the carbon skeleton on its surface effectively limits the volume expansion of the Bi nanospheres; Bi@C electrodes maintain a stable structure even after 2000 cycles.</p>
            
            <p>Encouraged by the satisfactory excellent rate capability and outstanding long-cycle performance of Bi@C, its LT electrochemical performance is evaluated by conducting constant charge–discharge measurements at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. At an extreme temperature of −40°C, the capacity of the Bi@C electrode had almost no decay at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> over the following 10 cycles (Figure <a href="#cey2531-fig-0003">3F</a>). As shown in Figure <a href="#cey2531-fig-0003">3G,H</a>, the Na//Bi@C half battery could still display a high capacity of 246 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> even at −40°C, which is equivalent to 96% of that at 25°C, indicating the outstanding LT performance of the Bi@C electrode. The LT data of 0°C and −20°C also show the excellent electrochemical performances of the Bi@C electrode, as shown in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>. However, the capacity of bulk Bi at LT dramatically decreased from 25°C to −40°C, as displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>. Nevertheless, the half-cell of this work has priority to the reported LT rechargeable cells in terms of current density, specific capacity, and capacity retention (the ratio of the capacity achieved at LT relative to that of 25°C), as revealed in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>. The fast sodium storage of Bi@C electrodes has achieved superior rate capability, outstanding long-cycle property, and excellent LT performance, which may be attributed to its unique core-shell structure with uniform nanosphere morphology and the applied electrolyte.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0044_R_d66667777e955" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Adv Energy Mater">44</a></sup></span> Nanosized materials have a larger electrolyte contact area, and the carriers can easily diffuse on the surface of the nanostructure, which is conducive to improving the rate performance of SIBs. The unique core-shell structure can alleviate volume expansion problems and maintain structural stability, thus achieving a long-cycle life. In summary, the nanosized Bi@C anode with a core-shell structure for SIBs demonstrated superior rate behavior, ultrahigh stability, and outstanding LT performance, thereby further confirming its promising practical application potential.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cey2531-sec-0100">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cey2531-sec-0100-title">3.3 The investigation of sodium-storage mechanisms</h3>
            
            <p>To achieve a more comprehensive understanding of the sodiation/desodiation mechanisms of Bi@C, we first performed in situ XRD measurements on Na//Bi half cells to monitor the crystalline structure change of the Bi@C electrode (Figure <a href="#cey2531-fig-0004">4A</a>). During the first discharge process, the peak at 26.8° disappeared due to the alloying reaction of Bi → NaBi, while the peak at 31.8° represented the characteristic peak of the NaBi phase. As the discharge progressed, the characteristic peak of the NaBi phase gradually diminished, while the peak intensity of Na<sub>3</sub>Bi gradually increased, indicating further alloying reaction of NaBi to generate Na<sub>3</sub>Bi. During the subsequent charging process, the characteristic peak of Na<sub>3</sub>Bi gradually weakened as the characteristic peak of NaBi emerged and gradually strengthened. This phenomenon was attributed to the dealloying reaction of Na<sub>3</sub>Bi, leading to the formation of NaBi. Upon further charging, the diffraction peak reversibly recovers back to its original place in Bi, but the intensity of NaBi is gradually weakened, suggesting the high reversibility of Bi during alloying/dealloying reaction processes. Additionally, the alloying/dealloying mechanism of the Bi@C anode in SIBs is illustrated in Figure <a href="#cey2531-fig-0004">4B</a>. In conclusion, the in situ XRD data have delivered impressive rechargeability of the Bi@C electrode. A two-step alloying/dealloying reaction has occurred during charge/discharge in the Bi@C electrode, and the reaction mechanism is consistent with the previous research.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0017_R_d66667777e982" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv Mater">17</a>, <a href="#cey2531-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0040_R_d66667777e982" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Small">40</a>, <a href="#cey2531-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0045_R_d66667777e982" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - J Power Sources">45</a></sup></span></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cey2531-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/629dd680-0b90-4c3a-853c-eae0066a77b3/cey2531-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/629dd680-0b90-4c3a-853c-eae0066a77b3/cey2531-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3e07e9a7-fdd0-4117-b2dd-2adaad57c72d/cey2531-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/629dd680-0b90-4c3a-853c-eae0066a77b3/cey2531-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

            </section>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cey2531-sec-0110">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cey2531-sec-0110-title">3.4 Dynamic analysis and theoretical simulations</h3>
            
            <p>To further shed light on the sodium ion storage kinetics of the sodiation/desodiation process at LT, a galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was carried out. As shown in Figure <a href="#cey2531-fig-0005">5A</a>, the GITT curves of the Bi@C electrode are displayed during the discharge process at 25°C and −40°C by discharging Bi@C at a current of 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup> for 20 min followed by a relaxation time of 80 min. The diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/febf0f4e-4676-4a5a-9fc2-41b2a9029a1a/cey2531-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:10973923:media:cey2531:cey2531-math-0001" location="graphic/cey2531-math-0001.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mrow>
                        <msub>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">D</mi>
                           <msup>
                              <mi>Na</mi>
                              <mo>+</mo>
                           </msup>
                        </msub>
                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex"> ${{\rm{D}}}_{{\mathrm{Na}}^{+}}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>) in Bi@C is calculated by solving Fick's second law.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0024_R_d66667777e1032" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv Energy Mater">24</a>, <a href="#cey2531-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0046_R_d66667777e1032" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Adv Energy Mater">46</a>-<a href="#cey2531-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0048_R_d66667777e1032" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Angew Chem Int Ed">48</a></sup></span> As shown in Figure <a href="#cey2531-fig-0005">5B</a>, the relative changes of <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/c3f89209-2885-48ca-97a7-b596b0845690/cey2531-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:10973923:media:cey2531:cey2531-math-0002" location="graphic/cey2531-math-0002.png">

                     <mrow>

                        </msub>
                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex"> ${{\rm{D}}}_{{\mathrm{Na}}^{+}}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> in the Bi@C anode mainly vary between 10<sup>−8</sup> and 10<sup>−13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> at −40°C and 25°C, indicating that the <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/9581b44f-63f8-42a3-baa9-e13a01e25e86/cey2531-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:10973923:media:cey2531:cey2531-math-0003" location="graphic/cey2531-math-0003.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mrow>
                        <msub>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">D</mi>
                           <msup>
                              <mi>Na</mi>
                              <mo>+</mo>

                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex"> ${{\rm{D}}}_{{\mathrm{Na}}^{+}}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> of Bi@C display temperature independence at 25°C and −40°C.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cey2531-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/54c81b3c-1485-4bfd-8c54-ff544282e27b/cey2531-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/54c81b3c-1485-4bfd-8c54-ff544282e27b/cey2531-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a9cee73f-fc5c-4f1b-b25b-116a250a2df7/cey2531-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/54c81b3c-1485-4bfd-8c54-ff544282e27b/cey2531-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cey2531-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcey2.531" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">(A) GITT profiles of the discharging process at different temperatures of Bi@C. (B) The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of Bi@C at 25°C and −40°C. (C) The Nyquist plots of the Bi@C//Na half cells at different temperatures. (D) The Arrhenius plots of <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> to estimate the activation energy of the Bi@C and bulk Bi. (E) Ex situ FTIR spectra of Bi@C//Na half-cell. (F) Illustration of Na<sup>+</sup>-diglyme cointercalation behavior in Bi@C. The charge density difference of pure (G) Bi and (H) Bi/C, where the yellow and blue stand for electron accumulation and depletion, respectively. (I) The adsorption energy of Na atoms on the pure Bi and Bi/C. The diagrams of sodium migration path on the pure (J) Bi and (K) Bi/C interface. (L) The migration energy barriers in the Bi/C interface and pure Bi. C, Bi, and Na atoms are presented by brown, purple, and yellow balls, respectively.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Meanwhile, the smaller contact angles in the NaPF<sub>6</sub>-diglyme electrolyte by contrasting with the bulk Bi electrode are revealed in the insets of Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>. The excellent electrolyte permeability and ionic conductivity of Bi@C electrode at LT are mainly ascribed to the unique structure of anodes and the adaptable electrolyte. Meanwhile, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements of Bi@C and bulk Bi electrodes were conducted (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>). By fitting the results with the equivalent circuit (inset in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>), the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) of Bi@C is determined to be approximatively 3 Ω while that of bulk Bi is be approximatively 80 Ω, demonstrating that the Bi@C electrode has faster electronic transport. The <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value from the Nyquist plots (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>) remains unchanged with the cycle number increasing, which manifests that the Bi@C electrode has excellent cycle stability.</p>
            
            <p>It is worth noting that the slow Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation process is considered to be the primary hindrance to the LT performance of SIBs, which is closely related to the activation energy of the reaction.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0049_R_d66667777e1129" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Chem Eng J">49</a>, <a href="#cey2531-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0050_R_d66667777e1129" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Adv Energy Mater">50</a></sup></span> To measure the activation energy during the Na<sup>+</sup> (dis)charge process, EIS measurements were carried out from −40°C to 25°C. As shown in Figure <a href="#cey2531-fig-0005">5C</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, the semicircle in the high-frequency region may contain the impedance of the SEI film, while the semicircle in the medium-frequency region corresponds to the charge transfer impedance at the SEI film/Bi@C or the SEI film/bulk Bi electrodes interface, which are denoted by <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0016_R_d66667777e1147" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Angew Chem Int Ed">16</a></sup></span> The <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub>, with its tiny variation from 25°C to −40°C for Bi@C electrodes, is not the major factor affecting the dynamics of LT charge transfer.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0051_R_d66667777e1154" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Nat Energy">51</a>, <a href="#cey2531-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0052_R_d66667777e1154" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Nano Energy">52</a></sup></span> Instead, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of the Bi@C electrode increases significantly from 25°C to −40°C and might be the determining step for the LT charge transfer, which was also presented in previous reports of LT SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0053_R_d66667777e1160" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Adv Mater">53</a></sup></span> Furthermore, the activation energy (denoted by <i>E</i><sub>a</sub>) values between Bi@C and bulk Bi in half batteries are estimated from the Arrhenius plot (Equation <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>).<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0016_R_d66667777e1173" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Angew Chem Int Ed">16</a>, <a href="#cey2531-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0024_R_d66667777e1173" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv Energy Mater">24</a></sup></span> As shown in Figure <a href="#cey2531-fig-0005">5D</a>, the estimated <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> for Bi@C is found to be 32.2 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>, which is much lower than the <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> for bulk Bi (60.8 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>). The low <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> for Bi@C suggests that the regulated core-shell nanostructure by carbon-coated frameworks of Bi@C anode can effectively reduce the reaction activation energy of Na<sup>+</sup>.</p>
            
            <p>To further gain insights into the causing of low <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> of Bi@C, ex situ Fourier-transform infrared (FTIR) tests were performed to characterize the Bi@C electrodes at different sodiation states. Significantly, the peak for the diglyme solvent at 836 cm<sup>−1</sup> is observed after dissolving 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> into the diglyme compared to the electrolyte,<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0048_R_d66667777e1206" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Angew Chem Int Ed">48</a></sup></span> as shown in Figure <a href="#cey2531-fig-0005">5E</a>. After discharging to 0.01 V, the characteristic peak of 836 cm<sup>−1</sup> appears in the FTIR spectra of the Bi@C electrode, indicating the existence of Na<sup>+</sup>-diglyme. However, the FTIR spectra of the Bi@C electrode after charging to 1.5 V display no characteristic signals of 836 cm<sup>−1</sup>, as well as the initial electrode. Obviously, the Bi@C electrode presents Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent cointercalation behavior in 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in diglyme electrolyte at the charge/discharge process, which supports the GITT and EIS results. Therefore, the fast reaction kinetics of SIBs at LT is dominated by Na<sup>+</sup>-diglyme cointercalation behavior, along with the unique nanostructure of the Bi@C electrode (Figure <a href="#cey2531-fig-0005">5F</a>). The phenomenon of Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent cointercalation behavior in ether-based electrolytes at LT is contributed to a large layer spacing (3.979 Å) of Bi, which is similar to the case of graphite electrodes.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0054_R_d66667777e1231" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Adv Mater">54</a>, <a href="#cey2531-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0055_R_d66667777e1231" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Adv Funct Mater">55</a></sup></span> It is worth noting that the desolvation step is the most significant and energy-consuming step in the diffusion process. As a result, the special Na<sup>+</sup>-diglyme cointercalation behavior can effectively reduce the activation energy and accelerate the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics, enabling the excellent LT performance of the Bi@C electrode.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0049_R_d66667777e1238" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Chem Eng J">49</a>, <a href="#cey2531-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0056_R_d66667777e1238" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Adv Mater">56</a></sup></span> Meanwhile, the synergistic effect of the electrolyte and the structural design of anode materials to maintain superior sodium storage at LT.</p>
            
            <p>The components of SEI layers were analyzed by ex situ XPS spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>). Similar organic–inorganic hybrid SEI structures are formed on Bi@C and bulk Bi electrodes during cycling. Compared with bulk Bi, an obvious increase of –C–H<sub>2</sub>– peak at 283.4 eV in the C 1s spectra on Bi@C after discharge/charge suggests the formation of sodium alkoxides (RCH<sub>2</sub>ONa), which is derived from the diglyme molecules and is important for the interfacial stability of Bi@C electrode.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0017_R_d66667777e1251" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv Mater">17</a>, <a href="#cey2531-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0057_R_d66667777e1251" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Adv Mater">57</a></sup></span> Besides, the components of SEI film in the ether-based electrolyte are mainly sodium alkoxides (R–O–Na), sodium carbonate (Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>), and sodium fluoride (NaF). NaF is a component that facilitates the rapid diffusion of sodium ions through the SEI layer, and it is formed by the reduction of the PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anion in the electrolyte on the electrode surface.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0058_R_d66667777e1262" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Adv Mater">58</a></sup></span> As revealed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14C</a>, the SEI layer of Bi@C surfaces shows higher NaF concentration than that of bulk Bi, indicating the rapid diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup>. To understand this phenomenon, a theoretical analysis based on the first principle and DFT simulations of the absorption of Na<sup>+</sup> and the energy barriers of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion on Bi and Bi/C was conducted.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0059_R_d66667777e1275" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Adv Funct Mater">59</a></sup></span> As shown in Figure <a href="#cey2531-fig-0005">5G,H</a>, when the Na is located between Bi and C, the charge accumulation (yellow) can be observed, which illustrates the obvious electron transfer within Na and Bi/C and further proves the strong interaction between them. Based on the simulation result (Figure <a href="#cey2531-fig-0005">5I</a>), the Na between the carbon layer and Bi has much stronger adsorption energy (−4.33 eV) in comparison with the pure Bi (−3.23 eV). In general, higher adsorption energy values in the atomic structure usually correspond to stronger attractive propensities.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0035_R_d66667777e1284" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Small">35</a></sup></span> The higher adsorption energy value of Bi/C indicates the strengthened reaction process when Na is involved. To further evaluate the mobility of Na atoms on Bi/C and pure Bi, the Na diffusion energy calculations on Bi/C and pure Bi were conducted, as shown in Figure <a href="#cey2531-fig-0006">6J–L</a>. The diffusion energy barrier of Na atoms on Bi/C (167 meV) was much lower than that of pure Bi (275 meV) and other Bi-based anode materials, evidencing higher mobilities of Na atoms and faster Na storage kinetics for the Bi/C electrode in SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#cey2531-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0038_R_d66667777e1291" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv Mater">38</a>, <a href="#cey2531-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2531-bib-0060_R_d66667777e1291" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Nano Lett">60</a></sup></span> Therefore, the DFT calculation results reveal that the higher adsorption energy and the lower diffusion energy barrier of Bi/C are beneficial to enhancing&nbsp;the reaction kinetics of SIBs, supporting the above experimental data in this work.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cey2531-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c440a0e3-849b-47eb-81cf-9c9ba0954f9c/cey2531-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c440a0e3-849b-47eb-81cf-9c9ba0954f9c/cey2531-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3656e94b-28a9-44d9-87ae-482c512315fa/cey2531-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c440a0e3-849b-47eb-81cf-9c9ba0954f9c/cey2531-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cey2531-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcey2.531" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of Bi@C//NVP full batteries. (A) The schematic diagram of the Bi@C//NVP full battery. (B) Galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of the Bi@C//NVP full batteries at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. (C) Cycling performance of Bi@C//NVP full batteries at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. (D) Rate performance of Bi@C//NVP full batteries. (E) Charge/discharge profiles at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> at different temperatures. (F) Comparison of different-temperature rate performance at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cey2531-sec-0120">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cey2531-sec-0120-title">3.5 Electrochemical characterizations of the Bi@C//NVP full batteries</h3>
            
            <p>To further evaluate the practical application potential of the Bi@C electrode, the full battery with Bi@C as the anode and NVP as the cathode was assembled, and a schematic illustration is shown in Figure <a href="#cey2531-fig-0006">6A</a>. The characteristics of NVP powder and the electrochemical properties of NVP//Na half-cell have been shown in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>. Accordingly, the specific capacities of the Bi@C//NVP full batteries were calculated based on the mass of the Bi@C. The Bi@C//NVP full batteries exhibited two flat redox reaction potentials (2.6/2.8 V), corresponding to the voltage differences between the discharge and charge plateaus of the cathode/anode (Figures <a href="#cey2531-fig-0006">6B</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>). Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> shows the CV curves for the first five cycles of the Bi@C//NVP full battery at 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. The charge/discharge profiles of different cycles have high overlap, evidencing that the Bi@C//NVP full battery has outstanding reversibility and cycling stability. Moreover, a high discharge-specific capacity of 270 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>&nbsp;was maintained at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> for 50 cycles, demonstrating outstanding cycling stability (Figures <a href="#cey2531-fig-0006">6C</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>). Even at a high current rate of 5 A g<sup>−1</sup> at 25°C, the Bi@C//NVP full battery still delivered a high-rate capacity of 263 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, revealing outstanding rate capability (Figure <a href="#cey2531-fig-0006">6D</a>).</p>
            
            <p>The LT performance of the Bi//NVP full batteries was further studied by conducting galvanostatic charge/discharge tests at various temperatures. The charge/discharge voltage curves at a discharge current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> under varying temperatures are displayed in Figure <a href="#cey2531-fig-0006">6E</a>, and the corresponding LT rate performance is shown in Figure <a href="#cey2531-fig-0006">6F</a>. At 25°C, the Bi@C//NVP full cells exhibited a discharge capacity of 270 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#cey2531-fig-0006">6E</a>). When the temperature was reduced to −10°C, −20°C, and −40°C, the Bi@C//NVP full batteries delivered a discharge capacity of 183, 160, and 95 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Remarkably, the Bi@C//NVP full battery exhibited steady cycle performance in the LT test, confirming its potential usage in LT rechargeable battery applications.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cey2531-sec-0130">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cey2531-sec-0130-title">4 CONCLUSIONS</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, core-shell structured Bi@C nanospheres were fabricated via a one-step chemical precipitation and calcination pathway with NTCDA as the carbon precursor. The Bi@C anode for SIBs delivered notable cycle stability with a capacity retention of 99% after 2000 cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> and remarkable rate capability (324 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>). Based on the detailed characterization, dynamic analysis, and DFT simulations, we reveal that the electrochemical performance under LT is attributed to the following reasons. (i) The unique core-shell nanosphere structure of Bi@C electrode material alleviates alleviates volume expansion problems to maintain structural stability and enhances electronic conductivity to accelerate reaction kinetics. (ii) The rational electrolyte ensures Na<sup>+</sup>-diglyme cointercalation into Bi@C electrode during the discharge process, and then the outstanding LT performances of Bi@C//Na half batteries at −40°C were realized, with only a 5% capacity decay rate lower than that of room temperature. The DFT theoretical simulations further confirmed the positive effect of the carbon frameworks on enhancing the adsorption and diffusion properties of Na<sup>+</sup>. Furthermore, GITT, EIS, and ex situ FTIR tests demonstrated that low charge transfer activation energy (32.2 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>) contributed to nearly temperature-independent diffusion coefficients of Na<sup>+</sup> during the Na<sup>+</sup>-diglyme cointercalation process. These traits enabled Bi@C//NVP full batteries to exhibit stable charging/discharging capability even at a temperature as low as −40°C. On the whole, this work proposes a significant insight into the rational design of high-performance electrode materials for SIBs in terms of practical large-scale energy storage in cold winter extremes.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cey2531-sec-0140-title"> ACKNOWLEDGMENTS</h2>
